We need to solve a combinatorial geometry problem. Let's restate:

We have n ≥ 3 integer. Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying:
(1) For all positive integers a and b with a+b ≤ n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines.
(2) Exactly k of the n lines are sunny. Sunny: a line not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or line x+y=0. So sunny lines are those with slope not 0, not infinite, not -1. So slopes ≠ 0, ∞, -1. So they are lines that are not horizontal, vertical, or with slope -1.

We need to find all possible k (depending on n) for which such a configuration exists.

Interpretation: We have a set of n distinct lines. They must cover all integer lattice points (a,b) with a,b positive integers and a+b ≤ n+1. So these are points in the first quadrant (including axes? a,b positive integers, so a≥1,b≥1) with sum ≤ n+1. So there are many points: for each s from 2 to n+1, points with a+b=s, a,b≥1. So total points = sum_{s=2}^{n+1} (s-1) = (n)(n+1)/2? Actually sum_{s=2}^{n+1} (s-1) = sum_{t=1}^{n} t = n(n+1)/2. So there are N = n(n+1)/2 points.

We need n lines covering these points. Each line can contain many points. The lines are distinct. Condition (2): exactly k of these lines are sunny (i.e., not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1). So the other n-k lines are not sunny: they are either horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. But note: lines can be parallel to these forbidden directions. So the non-sunny lines are lines that are either horizontal (y = constant), vertical (x = constant), or with slope -1 (y = -x + c). But they could also be any line that is parallel to one of these? Actually definition: a line is sunny if it is not parallel to any of the x-axis, y-axis, or line x+y=0. So if a line is parallel to x-axis (horizontal), it is not sunny. Similarly vertical, or parallel to x+y=0 (slope -1). So non-sunny lines are exactly those that are horizontal, vertical, or have slope -1. So they are lines of the form y = c (c constant), x = c, or y = -x + c.

Now we need to cover all points (a,b) with a,b positive integers, a+b ≤ n+1. The points are in a triangular region. We need to select n lines (distinct) such that every point lies on at least one line. And exactly k of them are sunny.

We need to determine all possible k for given n. Likely answer: k can be any integer from 0 to n, except possibly some restrictions? But we need to check feasibility.

Observation: The points are many, but we have only n lines. So each line must cover many points. The maximum number of points a line can cover among these points? For a given line, the points (a,b) on it must satisfy some linear equation. Since a,b are positive integers with sum ≤ n+1, the line can pass through at most something. For horizontal line y = c, it covers points with b=c and a≥1, a+c ≤ n+1 => a ≤ n+1-c. So number of points = max(0, n+1-c). Since c positive integer? Actually b positive integer, so c≥1. So horizontal lines cover at most n points (when c=1, a from 1 to n, gives n points). Similarly vertical x=c covers points with a=c, b≥1, b ≤ n+1-c => at most n points. Slope -1 lines: y = -x + c, then a+b=c, so points with a,b≥1 and a+b=c. For c from 2 to n+1, number of points = c-1. So maximum points on a slope -1 line is n (when c=n+1, gives n points). So each non-sunny line can cover at most n points. Sunny lines can cover many points as well, but they are not restricted to those specific slopes. However, a sunny line can also cover many points, possibly up to n? Actually consider a line with slope m not 0, ∞, -1. It can contain at most one point from each row? Not exactly; it can intersect many integer points. For example, line y = 2x passes through (1,2), (2,4), (3,6), ... but within our triangle a+b ≤ n+1, the points are limited. The maximum number of integer points on a line within that triangle could be something like floor((n+1)/2) maybe? Let's analyze.

We have n lines covering N points. Since N = n(n+1)/2, the average number of points per line is (n+1)/2. So each line covers on average about (n+1)/2 points. So it's plausible that we can use lines that each cover many points.

We need to find all k such that there exists a set of n lines covering all points, with exactly k sunny lines. Since non-sunny lines are limited in number (n-k) and each covers at most n points, but we need to cover all points. The sunny lines can cover more points, but they are limited by geometry.

We need to determine constraints on k. Possibly k must be at least something, or at most something. Let's try small n to guess pattern.

n=3. Points: a,b positive, a+b ≤ 4. Points: (1,1), (1,2), (1,3), (2,1), (2,2), (3,1). That's 6 points. We need 3 lines covering all 6 points. Exactly k sunny lines. Determine possible k.

We can try to construct configurations. For n=3, we can use three lines: maybe two non-sunny and one sunny? Or all sunny? Or all non-sunny? Let's test.

Non-sunny lines: horizontal, vertical, slope -1. For n=3, possible lines: y=1 covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1) -> 3 points. y=2 covers (1,2),(2,2) -> 2 points. y=3 covers (1,3) -> 1 point. x=1 covers (1,1),(1,2),(1,3) -> 3 points. x=2 covers (2,1),(2,2) -> 2 points. x=3 covers (3,1) ->1 point. Slope -1: y=-x+2 covers (1,1) ->1 point. y=-x+3 covers (1,2),(2,1) ->2 points. y=-x+4 covers (1,3),(2,2),(3,1) ->3 points.

We need 3 lines covering all 6 points. Could we use three non-sunny lines? Possibly y=1 (covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1)), x=1 (covers (1,1),(1,2),(1,3)), and y=-x+4 (covers (1,3),(2,2),(3,1)). But note: (1,1) is covered by both y=1 and x=1; (1,2) by x=1; (1,3) by x=1 and y=-x+4; (2,1) by y=1; (2,2) by y=-x+4; (3,1) by y=1 and y=-x+4. So all points covered. Are these lines distinct? <solution>
We were unable to produce a complete proof. However, the strongest partial progress is as follows:
- For n=3, we can have k=0 (use three non-sunny lines: y=1, x=1, y=-x+4). We cannot have k=1 or 2 because we would need at least one sunny line, but then the other lines would have to cover all points, and it's impossible to cover all points with only 1 or 2 lines (since each line covers at most 3 points, and we have 6 points). So for n=3, possible k=0 only.
- For n=4, we can have k=0 (use four non-sunny lines: y=1, x=1, y=-x+4, and maybe another line like y=2? But y=2 covers (1,2),(2,2) only 2 points. Actually we need to cover all 10 points. Possibly we can use 4 non-sunny lines: y=1 (covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1)), x=1 (covers (1,1),(1,2),(1,3)), y=-x+4 (covers (1,3),(2,2),(3,1)), and y=2? That covers (1,2),(2,2) but (1,2) already covered, (2,2) already covered. Actually we need to cover (2,2) and (3,1) etc. The four lines might cover all points. Let's check: y=1 covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1). x=1 covers (1,1),(1,2),(1,3). y=-x+4 covers (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). So points covered: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(1,2),(1,3),(2,2). Missing: (2,3),(3,2)?? Actually (2,3) sum=5, not covered. (3,2) sum=5, not covered. (2,3) and (3,2) are points with sum 5. Also (1,? ) all covered. So we need to cover (2,3) and (3,2). Could we add a line y=-x+5? That covers (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) but (1,4) not in set. Actually (2,3) and (3,2) are in set. So we could use y=-x+5 as a fourth line. That line is also non-sunny (slope -1). So we have four lines: y=1, x=1, y=-x+4, y=-x+5. That covers all points? Check: y=1 covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1). x=1 covers (1,1),(1,2),(1,3). y=-x+4 covers (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). y=-x+5 covers (2,3),(3,2). So all 10 points covered. So k=0 is possible for n=4.
- For n=4, could we have k=1? Possibly yes. For example, use one sunny line and three non-sunny lines. But we need to check if we can cover all points with 4 lines, exactly one sunny. Might be possible. But we need to determine all possible k for general n. The problem likely expects an answer like: k can be any integer from 0 to n-1? Or maybe k can be any integer from 0 to n? Let's think more systematically.

We need to cover all points (a,b) with a,b positive integers and a+b ≤ n+1. There are N = n(n+1)/2 points. We have n lines. Each line can cover at most something. For a non-sunny line (horizontal, vertical, or slope -1), the maximum number of points it can cover is n (if it is y=1, it covers (1,1),(2,1),...,(n,1) but only those with a+b ≤ n+1? Actually (n,1) sum = n+1, so it's covered. So y=1 covers exactly n points: (1,1),(2,1),...,(n,1). Similarly x=1 covers n points: (1,1),(1,2),...,(1,n). A slope -1 line y = -x + c covers points with x+y=c. The maximum number of such points in the set is c-1 (since x≥1,y≥1). For c = n+1, it covers n points: (1,n),(2,n-1),...,(n,1). So each non-sunny line can cover at most n points. For a sunny line (slope not 0,∞,-1), what is the maximum number of points it can cover? Possibly less. For example, a line with slope 1: y = x + d. For d=0, it passes through (1,1),(2,2),..., but only those with a+b ≤ n+1. For a=b, sum=2a ≤ n+1 => a ≤ floor((n+1)/2). So it covers at most floor((n+1)/2) points. For a line with slope 2: y=2x+d, then x and y positive, sum ≤ n+1. The maximum number of integer points is limited. In general, a line with slope m (not 0,∞,-1) can cover at most something like O(n) but maybe less than n? Actually consider a line with slope 1/2: y = (1/2)x + d. For integer x, y integer only when x even. So it can cover at most about n/2 points. So sunny lines cover fewer points than non-sunny lines (which can cover up to n points). So to cover N points with n lines, we need enough lines that cover many points. The average number of points per line is N/n = (n+1)/2. So each line on average covers about (n+1)/2 points. Non-sunny lines can cover up to n points, which is larger than average. Sunny lines cover at most something like floor((n+1)/2) (if slope 1) or less. So it's plausible that we can use only non-sunny lines to achieve coverage, as we did for n=3,4. But the problem asks: determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines satisfying both conditions. So we need to find, for given n, which k (number of sunny lines) are achievable.

We can try to construct configurations with a given number of sunny lines. Perhaps we can always use n lines, all non-sunny, to cover all points. That would give k=0. So k=0 is always possible. Is k=1 possible? Possibly yes, by replacing one non-sunny line with a sunny line that still covers enough points. But we need to ensure that the remaining lines cover all points. Since we have n lines, and we want exactly one sunny line, we need to cover all points with n-1 non-sunny lines and one sunny line. The total coverage of the non-sunny lines is at most (n-1)*n (if each covers n points). But we need to cover N points. The sunny line covers at most something. So we need to see if we can achieve coverage with 1 sunny line. For n=3, we already saw it's impossible because the sunny line can cover at most floor((n+1)/2)=2 points, and the other two non-sunny lines can cover at most 2n=6 points, but total points 6, so we need the sunny line to cover 0 points? Actually we need to cover all 6 points. If we have 2 non-sunny lines, they can cover at most 2n=6 points, but they might not cover all 6 because they might overlap. For n=3, the maximum coverage of 2 non-sunny lines is 6 if they are disjoint, but they can be chosen to be y=1 and x=1? That covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(1,2),(1,3) = 5 points. They overlap at (1,1). So for n=3, we have a configuration with exactly one sunny line: y=x. So k=1 is possible for n=3.
